<meta name="DC.Title" content="Genomic Foundation of Starch-to-Lipid Switch in Oleaginous Chlorella spp." />
<meta name="DC.Identifier" content="10.1104/pp.15.01174" />
<meta name="DC.Date" content="2015-12-01" />
<meta name="DC.Publisher" content="American Society of Plant Biologists" />
<meta name="DC.Rights" content="© 2015 American Society of Plant Biologists. All Rights Reserved." />
<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="restricted" />
<meta name="DC.Relation" content="10.1104/pp.15.01761" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="The ability to rapidly switch the intracellular energy storage form from starch to lipids is an advantageous trait for microalgae feedstock. To probe this mechanism, we sequenced the 56.8-Mbp genome of Chlorella pyrenoidosa FACHB-9, an industrial production strain for protein, starch, and lipids. The genome exhibits positive selection and gene family expansion in lipid and carbohydrate metabolism and genes related to cell cycle and stress response. Moreover, 10 lipid metabolism genes might be originated from bacteria via horizontal gene transfer. Transcriptomic dynamics tracked via messenger RNA sequencing over six time points during metabolic switch from starch-rich heterotrophy to lipid-rich photoautotrophy revealed that under heterotrophy, genes most strongly expressed were from the tricarboxylic acid cycle, respiratory chain, oxidative phosphorylation, gluconeogenesis, glyoxylate cycle, and amino acid metabolisms, whereas those most down-regulated were from fatty acid and oxidative pentose phosphate metabolism. The shift from heterotrophy into photoautotrophy highlights up-regulation of genes from carbon fixation, photosynthesis, fatty acid biosynthesis, the oxidative pentose phosphate pathway, and starch catabolism, which resulted in a marked redirection of metabolism, where the primary carbon source of glycine is no longer supplied to cell building blocks by the tricarboxylic acid cycle and gluconeogenesis, whereas carbon skeletons from photosynthesis and starch degradation may be directly channeled into fatty acid and protein biosynthesis. By establishing the first genetic transformation in industrial oleaginous C. pyrenoidosa , we further showed that overexpression of an NAD(H) kinase from Arabidopsis ( Arabidopsis thaliana ) increased cellular lipid content by 110.4%, yet without reducing growth rate. These findings provide a foundation for exploiting the metabolic switch in microalgae for improved photosynthetic production of food and fuels.

*   ### Glossary
    
    SHDP
    :   sequential heterotrophy-dilution-photoautotrophy
    cDNA
    :   complementary DNA
    HGT
    :   horizontal gene transfer
    GO
    :   Gene Ontology
    ACCase
    :   acetyl-CoA carboxylase
    TF
    :   transcriptional factor
    TCA
    :   tricarboxylic acid
    NCBI
    :   National Center for Biotechnology Information
    TRA
    :   transcript relative abundance
    FPKM
    :   fragments Per kilobase of exon model per million mapped fragments" />

<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;The ability to rapidly switch the intracellular energy storage form from starch to lipids is an advantageous trait for microalgae feedstock. To probe this mechanism, we sequenced the 56.8-Mbp genome of &lt;i&gt;Chlorella pyrenoidosa&lt;/i&gt; FACHB-9, an industrial production strain for protein, starch, and lipids. The genome exhibits positive selection and gene family expansion in lipid and carbohydrate metabolism and genes related to cell cycle and stress response. Moreover, 10 lipid metabolism genes might be originated from bacteria via horizontal gene transfer. Transcriptomic dynamics tracked via messenger RNA sequencing over six time points during metabolic switch from starch-rich heterotrophy to lipid-rich photoautotrophy revealed that under heterotrophy, genes most strongly expressed were from the tricarboxylic acid cycle, respiratory chain, oxidative phosphorylation, gluconeogenesis, glyoxylate cycle, and amino acid metabolisms, whereas those most down-regulated were from fatty acid and oxidative pentose phosphate metabolism. The shift from heterotrophy into photoautotrophy highlights up-regulation of genes from carbon fixation, photosynthesis, fatty acid biosynthesis, the oxidative pentose phosphate pathway, and starch catabolism, which resulted in a marked redirection of metabolism, where the primary carbon source of glycine is no longer supplied to cell building blocks by the tricarboxylic acid cycle and gluconeogenesis, whereas carbon skeletons from photosynthesis and starch degradation may be directly channeled into fatty acid and protein biosynthesis. By establishing the first genetic transformation in industrial oleaginous &lt;i&gt;C. pyrenoidosa&lt;/i&gt;, we further showed that overexpression of an NAD(H) kinase from Arabidopsis (&lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis thaliana&lt;/i&gt;) increased cellular lipid content by 110.4%, yet without reducing growth rate. These findings provide a foundation for exploiting the metabolic switch in microalgae for improved photosynthetic production of food and fuels.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="citation_reference" content="citation_journal_title=Biotechnol Biofuels;citation_author=J. Zhang;citation_author=Q. Hao;citation_author=L. Bai;citation_author=J. Xu;citation_author=W. <meta name="citation_reference" content="citation_journal_title=Journal of Phycology;citation_journal_abbrev=Journal of Phycology;citation_author=M. Zhekisheva;citation_author=S. Boussiba;citation_author=I. Khozin-Goldberg;citation_author=A. Zarka;citation_author=Z. Cohen;citation_title=Accumulation of oleic acid in Haematococcus pluvialis (Chlorophyceae) under nitrogen starvation or high light is correlated with that of astaxanthin esters;citation_pages=325-331;citation_volume=38;citation_year=2002;citation_issue=2;citation_doi=10.1046/j.1529-8817.2002.01107.x" />
<meta name="twitter:title" content="Genomic Foundation of Starch-to-Lipid Switch in Oleaginous Chlorella spp." />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="http://www.plantphysiol.org/sites/default/files/highwire/plantphysiol/169/4.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="The ability to rapidly switch the intracellular energy storage form from starch to lipids is an advantageous trait for microalgae feedstock. To probe this mechanism, we sequenced the 56.8-Mbp genome of Chlorella pyrenoidosa FACHB-9, an industrial production strain for protein, starch, and lipids. The genome exhibits positive selection and gene family expansion in lipid and carbohydrate metabolism and genes related to cell cycle and stress response. Moreover, 10 lipid metabolism genes might be originated from bacteria via horizontal gene transfer. Transcriptomic dynamics tracked via messenger RNA sequencing over six time points during metabolic switch from starch-rich heterotrophy to lipid-rich photoautotrophy revealed that under heterotrophy, genes most strongly expressed were from the tricarboxylic acid cycle, respiratory chain, oxidative phosphorylation, gluconeogenesis, glyoxylate cycle, and amino acid metabolisms, whereas those most down-regulated were from fatty acid and oxidative pentose phosphate metabolism. The shift from heterotrophy into photoautotrophy highlights up-regulation of genes from carbon fixation, photosynthesis, fatty acid biosynthesis, the oxidative pentose phosphate pathway, and starch catabolism, which resulted in a marked redirection of metabolism, where the primary carbon source of glycine is no longer supplied to cell building blocks by the tricarboxylic acid cycle and gluconeogenesis, whereas carbon skeletons from photosynthesis and starch degradation may be directly channeled into fatty acid and protein biosynthesis. By establishing the first genetic transformation in industrial oleaginous C. pyrenoidosa , we further showed that overexpression of an NAD(H) kinase from Arabidopsis ( Arabidopsis thaliana ) increased cellular lipid content by 110.4%, yet without reducing growth rate. These findings provide a foundation for exploiting the metabolic switch in microalgae for improved photosynthetic production of food and fuels.

*   ### Glossary
    
    SHDP
    :   sequential heterotrophy-dilution-photoautotrophy
    cDNA
    :   complementary DNA
    HGT
    :   horizontal gene transfer
    GO
    :   Gene Ontology
    ACCase
    :   acetyl-CoA carboxylase
    TF
    :   transcriptional factor
    TCA
    :   tricarboxylic acid
    NCBI
    :   National Center for Biotechnology Information
    TRA
    :   transcript relative abundance
    FPKM
    :   fragments Per kilobase of exon model per million mapped fragments" />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="The ability to rapidly switch the intracellular energy storage form from starch to lipids is an advantageous trait for microalgae feedstock. To probe this mechanism, we sequenced the 56.8-Mbp genome of Chlorella pyrenoidosa FACHB-9, an industrial production strain for protein, starch, and lipids. The genome exhibits positive selection and gene family expansion in lipid and carbohydrate metabolism and genes related to cell cycle and stress response. Moreover, 10 lipid metabolism genes might be originated from bacteria via horizontal gene transfer. Transcriptomic dynamics tracked via messenger RNA sequencing over six time points during metabolic switch from starch-rich heterotrophy to lipid-rich photoautotrophy revealed that under heterotrophy, genes most strongly expressed were from the tricarboxylic acid cycle, respiratory chain, oxidative phosphorylation, gluconeogenesis, glyoxylate cycle, and amino acid metabolisms, whereas those most down-regulated were from fatty acid and oxidative pentose phosphate metabolism. The shift from heterotrophy into photoautotrophy highlights up-regulation of genes from carbon fixation, photosynthesis, fatty acid biosynthesis, the oxidative pentose phosphate pathway, and starch catabolism, which resulted in a marked redirection of metabolism, where the primary carbon source of glycine is no longer supplied to cell building blocks by the tricarboxylic acid cycle and gluconeogenesis, whereas carbon skeletons from photosynthesis and starch degradation may be directly channeled into fatty acid and protein biosynthesis. By establishing the first genetic transformation in industrial oleaginous C. pyrenoidosa , we further showed that overexpression of an NAD(H) kinase from Arabidopsis ( Arabidopsis thaliana ) increased cellular lipid content by 110.4%, yet without reducing growth rate. These findings provide a foundation for exploiting the metabolic switch in microalgae for improved photosynthetic production of food and fuels.

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-article-citation article-page-title-citation" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="364" id="node364--2" data-pisa="plantphysiol;169/4/2444" data-pisa-master="plantphysiol;pp.15.01174" data-apath="/plantphysiol/169/4/2444.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-jcore-article-title-compact clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">
  
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-overline"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-article-type highwire-cite-metadata">Research Article</span><span class="separator-pipe"></span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-art-cat highwire-cite-metadata"><span class="wrapper">Articles</span></span></div>

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="item-list"><ul class="tabs inline panels-ajax-tab"><li class="first"><a href="/content/169/4/2444" class="panels-ajax-tab-tab" data-panel-name="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" data-target-id="highwire_article_tabs" data-entity-context="node:364" data-trigger="" data-url-enabled="1">Article</a><a href="/panels_ajax_tab/drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art/node:364/1" rel="nofollow" style="display:none" class="js-crawler-link"></a></li><li><a href="/content/169/4/2444/tab-figures-data" class="panels-ajax-tab-tab" data-panel-name="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_data" data-target-id="highwire_article_tabs" data-entity-context="node:364" data-trigger="tab-figures-data" data-url-enabled="1">Figures &amp; Data</a><a href="/panels_ajax_tab/drupal_pub_aspb_tab_data/node:364/1" rel="nofollow" style="display:none" class="js-crawler-link"></a></li><li><a href="/content/169/4/2444/tab-article-info" class="panels-ajax-tab-tab" data-panel-name="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_info" data-target-id="highwire_article_tabs" data-entity-context="node:364" data-trigger="tab-article-info" data-url-enabled="1">Info &amp; Metrics</a><a href="/panels_ajax_tab/drupal_pub_aspb_tab_info/node:364/1" rel="nofollow" style="display:none" class="js-crawler-link"></a></li><li class="last"><a href="/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444.full.pdf" class="panels-ajax-tab-tab" data-panel-name="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_pdf" data-target-id="highwire_article_tabs" data-entity-context="node:364" data-trigger="tab-pdf" data-url-enabled="1"><i class="icon-file-alt"></i> PDF</a><a href="/panels_ajax_tab/drupal_pub_aspb_tab_pdf/node:364/1" rel="nofollow" style="display:none" class="js-crawler-link"></a></li></ul></div>  </div>

  
  </div>
<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-drupal_pub_aspb_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><ul class="copyright-statement"><li class="fn" id="copyright-statement-1">© 2015 American Society of Plant Biologists. All Rights Reserved.</li></ul><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-5">The ability to rapidly switch the intracellular energy storage form from starch to lipids is an advantageous trait for microalgae feedstock. To probe this mechanism, we sequenced the 56.8-Mbp genome of <em>Chlorella pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9, an industrial production strain for protein, starch, and lipids. The genome exhibits positive selection and gene family expansion in lipid and carbohydrate metabolism and genes related to cell cycle and stress response. Moreover, 10 lipid metabolism genes might be originated from bacteria via horizontal gene transfer. Transcriptomic dynamics tracked via messenger RNA sequencing over six time points during metabolic switch from starch-rich heterotrophy to lipid-rich photoautotrophy revealed that under heterotrophy, genes most strongly expressed were from the tricarboxylic acid cycle, respiratory chain, oxidative phosphorylation, gluconeogenesis, glyoxylate cycle, and amino acid metabolisms, whereas those most down-regulated were from fatty acid and oxidative pentose phosphate metabolism. The shift from heterotrophy into photoautotrophy highlights up-regulation of genes from carbon fixation, photosynthesis, fatty acid biosynthesis, the oxidative pentose phosphate pathway, and starch catabolism, which resulted in a marked redirection of metabolism, where the primary carbon source of glycine is no longer supplied to cell building blocks by the tricarboxylic acid cycle and gluconeogenesis, whereas carbon skeletons from photosynthesis and starch degradation may be directly channeled into fatty acid and protein biosynthesis. By establishing the first genetic transformation in industrial oleaginous <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>, we further showed that overexpression of an NAD(H) kinase from Arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>) increased cellular lipid content by 110.4%, yet without reducing growth rate. These findings provide a foundation for exploiting the metabolic switch in microalgae for improved photosynthetic production of food and fuels.</p></div><p id="p-16">Microalgae are considered promising feedstock for sustainable, large-scale production of commodities such as food, feed, chemicals, materials, and biofuels (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">Chisti, 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">Hu et al., 2008</a>; <a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">Scott et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">Wijffels and Barbosa, 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">Ho et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">Savage, 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">Hildebrand et al., 2013</a>). Compared to traditional land crops such as corn (<em>Zea mays</em>), rice (<em>Oryza sativa</em>), and wheat (<em>Triticum aestivum</em>), one key feature of microalgae-based production is their ability to rapidly switch their energy storage forms from polysaccharides (e.g., starch) to lipids or to high-value compounds such as proteins under certain environmental stresses (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Sansawa and Endo, 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Fan et al., 2012b</a>; <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Li et al., 2014b</a>). This metabolic feature is of significance in commercialization of large-scale microalgal production systems for biofuels, as the switch between food and fuel production allows nimbleness and flexibility of operation in the volatile market. However, the molecular mechanism underlying such a metabolic switch in microalgae remains poorly understood (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">Merchant et al., 2012</a>).</p><p id="p-17"><em>Chlorella</em> spp. are a group of unicellular freshwater green microalgae that evolved over one billion years ago (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">Heckman et al., 2001</a>). They are among the most widely cultivated microalgae by mankind (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Klein-Marcuschamer et al., 2013</a>), with an annual net production of about 4,000 tons biomass worldwide. <em>Chlorella</em> spp. have served as a popular food supplement and animal feed in Asia, the United States, and Europe for centuries because of their high protein, lipid, and chlorophyll content (the highest amount among known plants; <a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Lee, 1997</a>; <a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">Spolaore et al., 2006</a>). However, strain improvement of industrial <em>Chlorella</em> spp. has been hindered by the lack of genomics research models. Moreover, although genetic modification of <em>Chlorella vulgaris</em> (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">Chow and Tung, 1997</a>) and <em>Chlorella ellipsoidea</em> (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">Chen et al., 2001</a>) were achieved, none of the <em>Chlorella</em> spp. strains shown to be amenable to transformation are of commercial interest (<a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">Tran et al., 2013</a>). Thus transformation of industrial production <em>Chlorella</em> spp. strains remains a key goal.</p><p id="p-18">One important feature of <em>Chlorella</em> spp. is their ability to support high biomass productivity under both autotrophic and heterotrophic culture modes (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">Gladue and Maxey, 1994</a>; <a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">Radmer and Parker, 1994</a>; <a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">Lee, 1997</a>; <a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Doucha and Livansky, 2012</a>). Under photoautotrophic conditions, most <em>Chlorella</em> spp. produce abundant proteins and lipids, but 1 to 2 weeks of cultivation is usually required from inoculation to harvest. Such a relatively long production cycle often results in low productivity and vulnerability to changes in the weather (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">Perez-Garcia et al., 2011</a>). However, when cultivated heterotrophically, they reach much higher biomass productivity (&gt;100 g L<sup>–1</sup>; <a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">Doucha and Livansky, 2012</a>) while accumulating significant levels of starch and much lower levels of lipids and proteins (<a id="xref-ref-63-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Sansawa and Endo, 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">Marsalkova et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">Choix et al., 2012</a>). Remarkably, when cells are shifted from heterotrophic to photoautotrophic conditions, a short duration (1 to 2 d depending on the weather) of illumination induces a prominent change in the intracellular profile, with marked decreases in starch yet 70% to 120% increases in lipids and proteins (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Fan et al., 2012a</a>, <a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">2012b</a>; <a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Han et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-36-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">Li et al., 2014b</a>), resulting in both high target product contents (lipids and proteins) and high biomass productivity (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S1</a>). Such a controlled switch that exploits the advantages of both autotrophy and heterotrophy shows potential in large-scale food and biofuel production. Similar switches have been found in many microalgae and have been exploited in cultivation (e.g., via high-light induction or change of nutritional mode) to improve the productivity of valuable cellular components such as astaxanthin, keto-carotenoid, and α-tocopherol (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">Ogbonna et al., 1999</a>; <a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">Hata et al., 2001</a>; <a id="xref-ref-86-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-86">Zhang and Lee, 2001</a>). Nevertheless, the molecular mechanism underlying the switch remains unclear.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="C. pyrenoidosa FACHB-9 as a research model for the starch-to-lipid switch and scalable production of lipids. A, Macromolecule profile under different culture models with heterotrophic cells as seed. B, Production of biomass and lipids by photoautotrophic growth in a 2-L column photobioreactor with 2% CO2. Comparisons of lipid production for heterotrophic and photoautotrophic seed for subsequent photoautotrophic culture are also presented. P-P indicates photoautotrophic culture with photoautotrophic cells as seed, and H-P indicates photoautotrophic culture with heterotrophic cells as seed. C, Dynamics of algal growth and cellular components during SHDP. Arrows represent the six sampling time points for mRNA-seq (0, 24, 72, 74, 80, and 96 h). Dcw, Dry cell weight." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1921510622" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;C. pyrenoidosa&lt;/em&gt; FACHB-9 as a research model for the starch-to-lipid switch and scalable production of lipids. A, Macromolecule profile under different culture models with heterotrophic cells as seed. B, Production of biomass and lipids by photoautotrophic growth in a 2-L column photobioreactor with 2% CO&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;. P-P indicates photoautotrophic culture with photoautotrophic cells as seed, and H-P indicates photoautotrophic culture with heterotrophic cells as seed. C, Dynamics of algal growth and cellular components during SHDP. Arrows represent the six sampling time points for mRNA-seq (0, 24, 72, 74, 80, and 96 h). Dcw, Dry cell weight.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F1.medium.gif" width="220" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 1." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F1.medium.gif" width="220" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/4410" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Figure 1.</span> <p id="p-19" class="first-child"><em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 as a research model for the starch-to-lipid switch and scalable production of lipids. A, Macromolecule profile under different culture models with heterotrophic cells as seed. B, Production of biomass and lipids by photoautotrophic growth in a 2-L column photobioreactor with 2% CO<sub>2</sub>. Comparisons of lipid production for heterotrophic and photoautotrophic seed for subsequent photoautotrophic culture are also presented. P-P indicates photoautotrophic culture with photoautotrophic cells as seed, and H-P indicates photoautotrophic culture with heterotrophic cells as seed. C, Dynamics of algal growth and cellular components during <a id="xref-def-1-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a>. Arrows represent the six sampling time points for mRNA-seq (0, 24, 72, 74, 80, and 96 h). Dcw, Dry cell weight.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-20">In this study, we established an oleaginous industrial <em>Chlorella pyrenoidosa</em> strain FACHB-9 as a model to investigate the genomic foundation of heterotrophy-to-photoautotrophy metabolism and the associated metabolic switch. We sequenced and annotated the 56.8-Mbp genome sequence of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9. Furthermore, we employed replicated deep messenger RNA sequencing (mRNA-seq) to track transcriptome dynamics during the transition from heterotrophy to photoautotrophy at six time points along the complete process of the metabolic switch from starch-centric to lipid-centric production, revealing the underpinning molecular machineries and mechanism. Finally, by demonstrating the first genetic transformation of industrial oleaginous <em>Chlorella</em> spp., we showed that introducing a NAD(H) kinase gene into FACHB-9 increased the lipid content by 45.3% to 110.4% but did not affect the growth rate of the host cells under either heterotrophic or photoautotrophic conditions. These findings provide a foundation for exploiting the metabolic switch in microalgae for improved photosynthetic production of food and fuels.</p><div class="section results-discussion" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">RESULTS AND DISCUSSION</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3><em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> as a Research Model for Large-Scale Switchable Production of Starch and Lipids</h3><p id="p-21"><em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> is one of the most heavily studied species in the <em>Chlorella</em> genus. Due to its rich protein content, relatively fast growth rate, and tolerance of a wide range of environmental conditions, <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> has been employed as commercial microalgal feedstock of proteins, lipids, and chlorophyll for both heterotrophic fermentation and outdoor mass cultivation (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">Running et al., 1994</a>; <a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">Li et al., 1998</a>; <a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">Mason, 2001</a>). Under a standalone heterotrophic culture mode, <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> strain FACHB-9 exhibited a competitive growth rate (average 2 g L<sup>–1</sup> h<sup>–1</sup>) and can reach greater than 150 g L<sup>–1</sup> dry cell weight (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S2</a>), which is comparable to a commercial yeast (<em>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</em>) fermentation system (approximately 130 g L<sup>–1</sup>; <a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">Chen, 1996</a>). In addition, the maximal biomass of the strain reached 4.2 g L<sup>–1</sup> after 13-d photoautotrophic growth using heterotrophic cells as seed, with the intracellular neutral lipid content increasing from 3.5% to 23.6% (1.91 and 1.66 times higher in biomass and lipid productivity, respectively, than with photoautotrophic seed; <a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1B</a>). These observations have allowed us to develop a process for <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> cultivation called sequential heterotrophy-dilution-photoautotrophy (<a id="xref-def-1-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Fan et al., 2012b</a>).</p><p id="p-22"><a id="xref-def-1-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> consists of two phases: first, the heterotrophic culture mode for biomass accumulation, and subsequently, the photoautotrophic culture mode for production of target metabolites (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>). In the heterotrophic mode, which lasts several days, Glc is gradually depleted while cell density steadily increased, resulting in accumulation of cellular starch to approximately 40% of dry cell weight (<a id="xref-fig-1-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1, A and C</a>). This is followed by dilution, which is critical to improve light penetration of high-density cultures (e.g., &gt;5 g L<sup>–1</sup>) before moving into the photoautotrophic mode. After the dilution, treatments of heterotrophically growing cells with a sustained high light irradiation of 250 μmol m<sup>–2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> for 24 h resulted in a marked increases in lipid, protein, and chlorophyll content but marked decreases in starch (<a id="xref-fig-1-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S1</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Fan et al., 2012b</a>). These <a id="xref-def-1-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a>-cultivated cells reach a state in which the metabolite profile is as rich in target products (lipid, protein, and chlorophyll) as that of autotrophically grown cells, yet the cultivation cycle is cut from the typical 1 to 2 weeks under the autotrophic mode to 3 to 5 d under <a id="xref-def-1-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> (<a id="xref-fig-1-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1A</a>). The high productivity demonstrated capability of commercial-scale cultivation and flexibility to switch cellular metabolites suggests <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 as an advantageous research model for the switchable production of starch, protein, and lipids.</p></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Genomic Foundation of Starch and Lipid Production</h3><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h4>General Feature of the Genome</h4><p id="p-23">The 56.6-Mbp nuclear genome of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 was assembled from a combination of 15× shotgun and 10× paired-end sequence coverage of Roche 454 Titanium (average read length of 400 bp; <a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table I</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S1</a>). Half of the assembled genome sequences are contained in nine scaffolds, each longer than 1.39 Mbp. The <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> nuclear genome is slightly larger than <em>Chlorella variabilis</em> NC64A (46.2 Mbp; <a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">Blanc et al., 2010</a>) and considerably larger than <em>Chlorella protothecoides</em> (22.9 Mbp; <a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Gao et al., 2014</a>), with <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> predicted to contain more genes (10,284 as opposed to 9,791 and 7,039 genes, respectively; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S2</a>), which suggests that great changes have taken place in the size of the genome after the diversification in <em>Chlorella</em> genus. This may be due to the different number of unique genes, multicopy genes, and genome duplication /rearrangements among close relatives (<a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">Gao et al., 2014</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/4447/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li><li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/4447/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li></ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table I.</span> <span class="caption-title">General features of C. pyrenoidosa FACHB-9 genome</span><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-24">To produce a high-quality map of gene structures, total RNA samples from both heterotrophic and autotrophic culture conditions were pooled and sequenced on 454 Titanium (533,300 raw reads produced; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S1</a>) and assembled into 13,018 unique complementary DNA (<a id="xref-def-2-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a>) isotigs. Furthermore, to detect rare transcripts, 187,503,403 raw reads of mRNA collected from <a id="xref-def-1-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> culture conditions and six time-points were collected (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S3</a>). All of the <a id="xref-def-2-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a> reads that passed quality control were used for transcript-based gene prediction. The vast majority (97%) of the predicted genes were supported by these mRNA-seq reads (fragments per kilobase of exon model per million mapped fragments [<a id="xref-def-10-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">FPKM</a>] value &gt; 1; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S1</a>), and 89% of the predicted proteins were complete models with both start and stop codons (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table I</a>).</p><p id="p-25">The predicted <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> protein-coding genes are rich in introns, averaging nearly nine introns per gene (the largest in known chlorophytes; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S2</a>) and only 1.1% intron-free genes (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table I</a>). Repeated sequences (such as retroelements, DNA transposons, simple repeats, and low-complexity sequences) made up merely 3.1% of the whole genome (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S4</a>), suggesting a much lower repeat content than <em>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</em> (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">Merchant et al., 2007</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h4>Genomic Features of C. pyrenoidosa That Drive Metabolic Versatility</h4><p id="p-26">Green algae have a complex phylogenetic history and constitute a large resource of genetic and metabolic diversity (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">Falkowski et al., 2004</a>). A whole-genome phylogeny of Chlorophyta species (consistent with 18S rDNA-based phylogeny; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S3</a>) was constructed from the 1,685 single-copy orthologous groups identified from seven sequenced green algal genomes (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Results S1</a>). Mapping these sets of single-copy orthologs allowed the selective pressure to be traced at every branch of chlorophyte evolution. At the Chlorophyta level, all 52 functional categories of genes are strongly constrained, with median estimates of ω (the <em>K</em><sub>a</sub>/<em>K</em><sub>s</sub> ratio of nonsynonymous to synonymous nucleotide divergence) much less than 1 (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>). The most constrained biological processes include photosynthesis, organelle organization, and protein phosphorylation, whereas the least constrained ones are lipid metabolism, phospholipid biosynthesis, glycolysis, and carbohydrate metabolism. When compared at the genus level (<em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 and <em>C. variabilis</em> NC64A), the most constrained processes are protein folding, the ubiquitin-dependent protein catabolic process, and cellular component organization, and the least constrained are tRNA aminoacylation for protein translation, the biosynthesis process, and photosynthesis (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S4</a>). Most of the functional categories, including lipid and carbohydrate metabolisms, are moderately constrained. Between the two <em>Chlorella</em> species, most genes are under strong negative selection (ω &lt; 0.1); however, 51 genes were found under positive selection with an average ω greater than 1 (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S5</a>). The largest subclasses are oxidation-reduction process, protein binding, and transport-related proteins. The functional and evolutionary implications of such quickly evolving enzymes appear crucial to driving the diverse metabolic capacity of <em>Chlorella</em> spp.</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogenetic analysis of the C. pyrenoidosa genome. The associated GO slim terms that have at least three genes are shown for biological process (A), molecular function (B), and cellular component (C). rRNA, Ribosomal RNA.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="216"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 3." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="216"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/4203" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 3.</span> <p id="p-28" class="first-child">Selection pressure on protein-coding sequences in green algae. For each of the 1,685 seven-way single-copy orthologous gene sets identified among seven selected Chlorophyta species, Phylogenetic Analysis by Maximum Likelihood (PAML) model M0 was used to estimate a single ω (<em>K</em><sub>a</sub>/<em>K</em><sub>s</sub>, ratio of nonsynonymous to synonymous nucleotide divergence) that is fixed across the reconstructed whole-genome phylogeny. The associated <a id="xref-def-4-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GO</a> slim terms that have at least three genes are shown for biological process (A), molecular function (B), and cellular component (C). rRNA, Ribosomal RNA.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-29">To probe the evolutionary forces behind the expanded starch and lipid metabolic gene repertoire, phylogenetic analysis was employed to investigate the horizontal gene transfer (<a id="xref-def-3-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a>) events in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> genome. Among the 231 genes related to starch metabolism (49 genes), fatty acid biosynthesis (45 genes), glycerolipid biosynthesis (48 genes), lipid activation (47 genes), and lipase (42 genes), 10 genes (all were related to the lipid metabolism, accounting for 5.5%) might have originated from bacteria via <a id="xref-def-3-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a> (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Figs. S5 and S6</a>).</p><p id="p-30">Three genes encoding fatty acyl-CoA synthase (FACS; g11, g937, and g2922) identified were phylogenetically close to bacterial homologs, suggesting their possible evolutionary origin from bacteria via <a id="xref-def-3-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a>. From the starting point of phototrophic process, transcriptional levels of all of the three genes exhibited significant up-regulation, while transcriptional levels of the other six long-chain (LC)-FACS genes slightly increased or decreased. The synchronous expression pattern of the <a id="xref-def-3-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a> candidates FACS, along with the increased production of glycerolipids, indicates their important contribution in converting carbon flux from fatty acids into lipid.</p><p id="p-31">Glycerol-3-phosphate acyltransferase (GPAT) genes usually have stringent one to two copy numbers in microalgae, even in heterokonts <em>Nannochloropsis</em> spp., which have highly expanded gene dose in both fatty acid biosynthesis and triacylglycerol (TAG) assembly pathways (<a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">Vieler et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">Wang et al., 2014</a>). However, in <em>Chlorella</em> spp., in addition to the homologous gene inherited in microalgae, another GPAT gene (g2197) was identified and inferred as being originated from bacteria via <a id="xref-def-3-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a>. This gene was transcriptionally active and up-regulated in the late heterotrophic process, suggesting its potential roles in lipid metabolism.</p><p id="p-32">In addition to the above four <a id="xref-def-3-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a> candidates, one β-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier protein (ACP) reductase gene (g7443) in fatty acid biosynthesis pathway, two enoyl-CoA hydratase genes (g4146, g4040) in fatty acid β-oxidation pathway in mitochondrion, and three lipase genes (g3730, g7283, and g10256) were inferred as <a id="xref-def-3-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a> candidates. All of the three β-oxidation-related <a id="xref-def-3-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a> candidates were dramatically up-regulated in late heterotrophic process and in the first several hours of phototrophic process. Thus, the diverse evolutionary origin of the <em>Chlorella</em> spp. lipid biosynthesis genes has underlain their massive genetic pools and likely contributed to the extraordinary capacity for lipid accumulation.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h4>Genomic Basis of Carbohydrate and Starch Metabolism in C. pyrenoidosa</h4><p id="p-33">Polysaccharides, including starch, are one major sink for photosynthetic carbon in many microalgae. The <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> genome encodes a distinct functional profile in carbohydrate metabolism (as represented by Gene Ontology [<a id="xref-def-4-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GO</a>] terms) from those of <em>C. reinhardtii</em>, <em>Phaeodactylum tricornutum</em>, and <em>Nannochloropsis oceanica</em> (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S6</a>). In <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>, <a id="xref-def-4-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GO</a> terms in the carbohydrate metabolic process (GO:0005975) are prominently enriched, including the subcategories chitin metabolic process (GO:0006030), polysaccharide metabolic process (GO:0005976), lipopolysaccharide biosynthetic process (GO:0009103), and amino sugar metabolic process (GO:0006040). These findings suggest that carbohydrate metabolic machinery is a prominent feature of the <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> genome.</p><p id="p-34">Starch is a common form of polysaccharides and the major storage carbohydrate in plants and green algae (<a id="xref-ref-85-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-85">Zeeman et al., 2010</a>). In the <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> genome, for 12 of the 17 nodes along the starch metabolic pathway, multiple genes were found in each step (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S7</a>). Comparison of the gene doses in each step of the starch metabolic pathways among six sequenced green algal species and strains revealed that <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> harbors an expanded repertoire of genes involved in starch biosynthesis and degradation (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S8</a>), in which selective steps such as ADP-Glc pyrophosphorylase, glucan-water dikinase, and 1,4-α-glucan branching enzyme were highly elevated in gene dose, while isoamylase and starch phosphorylase were less variable and identical in gene dose to other green algae. This suggests that, in green algae, the evolution of starch synthesis pathways is characterized by conservation of pathway backbones and divergence of gene doses in selected pathway nodes.</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h4>Genomic Foundation of Lipid Metabolism</h4><p id="p-35">To probe the mechanism behind the robust accumulation of neutral lipids and the rapid starch to lipid switch in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>, we reconstructed the fatty acid biosynthesis, TAG assembly, and lipid activation/degradation pathways (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S9</a>). In <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>, glycerolipids biosynthesis is presumably carried out by two distinct pathways: the prokaryotic pathway (located in the chloroplast), which is widely distributed in prokaryotes, microalgae, and higher plants, and the eukaryotic pathway, which occurs in the endoplasmic reticulum (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">Shen et al., 2010</a>). Genes encoding both heteromeric (multisubunit; g2736, g8911, and g9519) and homomeric (multifunctional; g6472 and g6473) acetyl-CoA carboxylase (<a id="xref-def-5-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">ACCase</a>) were identified. Six putative LC-FACS genes were identified in the genome. Six copies of diacylglycerol acyltransferase (DGAT) genes were discovered in the <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> genome (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S9</a>). Five of the six <em>DGAT</em> genes encode putative type-2 DGATs, whereas the remaining one encodes type-1 DGAT (g7494). Previous research has indicated that there is an alternate pathway for TAG synthesis in yeast, plants (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">Dahlqvist et al., 2000</a>), and <em>Chlamydomonas</em> spp. (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">Riekhof and Benning, 2009</a>) that involves phospholipid:diacylglycerol acyltransferase (PDAT) to generate TAG using phospholipids as donors. One PDAT gene was identified in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> (g2349; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S9</a>).</p><p id="p-36">Comparison of <a id="xref-def-4-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GO</a> term profile in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> with those in <em>C. reinhardtii</em>, <em>P. tricornutum</em>, and <em>N. oceanica</em> revealed the overrepresentation in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> of the <a id="xref-def-4-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GO</a> terms for the phospholipid metabolic process (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S6</a>). Moreover, the expansion of several other gene families was apparent, which may contribute to the robust biomass and lipid production phenotypes of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>. These include genes involved in photosynthesis, responses to stress and other organisms (including bacteria and fungi), phosphorus utilization, and the cell cycle (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S6</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h4><a id="xref-def-6-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-6"><em>TF</em></a>s Involved in Starch and Lipid-Related Metabolic Pathways</h4><p id="p-37">Genome-wide identifications of transcriptional factors (<a id="xref-def-6-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s) by computational methods were reported for green algae such as <em>C. reinhardtii</em> and <em>Volvox carteri</em> (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Pérez-Rodríguez et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-87-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Zhang et al., 2011</a>), red algae such as <em>Galdieria sulphuraria</em> (<a id="xref-ref-51-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">Pérez-Rodríguez et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-87-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Zhang et al., 2011</a>), and Eustigmatophyceae strains such as <em>Nannochloropsis</em> spp. (<a id="xref-ref-79-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-79">Vieler et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">Hu et al., 2014</a>). Here, we performed such analysis in <em>Chlorella</em> spp. via the characteristic domains of plant <a id="xref-def-6-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s using the methods of PlantTFDB (<a id="xref-ref-87-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-87">Zhang et al., 2011</a>; <a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">Jin et al., 2014</a>). The results revealed 195 <a id="xref-def-6-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> (1.9% of the proteome; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S2</a>). Each predicted <a id="xref-def-6-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a> was then assigned into a specific <a id="xref-def-6-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a> family based on its DNA-binding domain. MYB, Cys3His zinc finger domain (C3H), and basic Leucine Zipper (bZIP) are the three largest <a id="xref-def-6-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s families, together accounting for 36% of all <a id="xref-def-6-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>. Orthologs in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> genome of higher plant <a id="xref-def-6-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s experimentally shown related to sugar signaling (e.g., WRKY, g3291; <a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">Sun et al., 2003</a>) and lipid accumulation (e.g. Dof, g1875; <a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Wang et al., 2007</a>) were identified.</p><p id="p-38">Fourteen and eight <a id="xref-def-6-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s were found putatively involved in the regulation of starch and lipid metabolism, respectively (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S2</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Methods S1</a>). In starch-related <a id="xref-def-6-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a> genes, eight <a id="xref-def-6-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a> families that include bZIP, C3H, SQUAMOSA promoter binding proteins, MYB, STERILE APETALA, GATA, B3 (the third basic domain in the VIVIPAROUS1), and ARR-B (a Myb-like DNA binding domain called ARRM [type B]) were identified, whereas in lipid-related <a id="xref-def-6-13" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s, seven families that include Calmodulin binding transcription factors, DNA binding with one finger, Heat stress transcription factors, GATA, MYB-related, CONSTANS-like, and Nodule inception-like were found. Notably, the Dof family of <a id="xref-def-6-14" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s, which contains a single C2C2-type zinc-finger-like motif, was shown to regulate in plants processes that include lipid accumulation and photosynthesis (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">Papi et al., 2002</a>; <a id="xref-ref-81-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Wang et al., 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">Shaw et al., 2009</a>). One of the family members, <em>Gmdof4</em>, was found involved in lipid synthesis in <em>Glycine max</em> by activating the <a id="xref-def-5-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">ACCase</a> gene (<a id="xref-ref-81-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Wang et al., 2007</a>). Moreover, total fatty acid and lipid content were significantly increased in <em>Gmdof4</em>-overexpressing Arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>; <a id="xref-ref-81-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">Wang et al., 2007</a>) and <em>C. ellipsoidea</em> (<a id="xref-ref-88-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-88">Zhang et al., 2014</a>). These <a id="xref-def-6-15" class="xref-list" href="#def-6">TF</a>s thus represent potential opportunities for engineering the regulatory network for enhanced starch or oil productivity.</p></div></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Transcriptome Dynamics during the Switch Process</h3><p id="p-39">To model the temporal process featuring the switch of the intracellular metabolite profile from one dominated by starch to one dominated by proteins and lipids, we changed the <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> cultivation mode from heterotrophy to photoautotrophy at 72 h (<a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">Fan et al., 2012a</a>, <a id="xref-ref-17-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">2012b</a>; <a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">Han et al., 2012</a>), followed by a 48-h light illumination. At each of the six time points during this <a id="xref-def-1-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> process, three biological replicates of separate culture experiments were each sampled for transcriptome analysis via deep mRNA sequencing and metabolite analysis (<a id="xref-fig-1-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>).</p><p id="p-40">A total of 187,503,403 trimmed paired-end reads (50 nucleotides in length after trimming, representing 22× average coverage of the genome at each sample; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S3</a>). Our results suggested that 1,578 and 1,491 nuclear genome-encoded genes were respectively up- and down-regulated greater than 3-fold at least one of the time points (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S7</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Sets S3 and S4</a>). A set of 75 transcripts was found whose expression levels together were able to distinguish the cellular state among the six time points in a highly reliable manner, suggesting a transcript-based strategy to quantitatively model the <a id="xref-def-1-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> process (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S8</a>).</p><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h4>Overview of the Switch Process</h4><p id="p-41">To determine the time-dependent differential gene expression when cells were transferred from heterotrophic growth to photoautotrophy, the temporal patterns of the relative transcript abundance over the six time points were grouped by <em>k</em>-means clustering, which revealed 16 distinct groups of protein-coding genes (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). During the course of the heterotrophic growth phase, the number of differentially expressed genes steadily increased, suggesting distinct cellular transcriptomic states during growth (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S7</a>). The number of genes differentially expressed in the phototrophic process at time point 80 h was significantly higher than the other sampling points (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S7</a>). This suggests a vigorous gene regulatory response in photoautotrophy, which can partly explain the rapid and massive changes in intracellular carbon storage forms within the first several hours (<a id="xref-fig-1-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Fan et al., 2012b</a>).</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Clustering results of gene expression patterns in C. pyrenoidosa when shifting heterotrophic cells to photoautotrophy. The shift from heterotrophy into photoautotrophy highlights the increased expression of genes involved in carbon fixation and photosynthesis that would be expected owing to light illumination (<a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>). Fatty acid biosynthesis, pentose phosphate pathway, and starch catabolism genes were also transcriptionally up-regulated.</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Overview of metabolic pathways and regulation during the heterotrophy to photoautotrophy transition in C. pyrenoidosa. Genes that are up- or down-regulated (compared with heterotrophy 72 h) are labeled in red and green, respectively. Deeper red and green indicates greater fold changes of differential expression." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1921510622" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Overview of metabolic pathways and regulation during the heterotrophy to photoautotrophy transition in &lt;em&gt;C. pyrenoidosa&lt;/em&gt;. Deeper red and green indicates greater fold changes of differential expression.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h4>How Was Starch Synthesized?</h4><p id="p-45">In <em>C. reinhardtii</em>, starch biosynthesis and degradation are under circadian clock control that appears uncorrelated with illumination (<a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">Ral et al., 2006</a>). However, there is evidence for starch turnover in <em>Chlorella</em> spp. during growth in response to light intensity or nutritional status (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">Rosen et al., 1986</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Fan et al., 2012b</a>). In our experiments, the starch content of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> cells showed a comparable decrease between the exponential and stationary phases of heterotrophic growth (48–72 h; <a id="xref-fig-1-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>; <a id="xref-ref-63-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">Sansawa and Endo, 2004</a>; <a id="xref-ref-17-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Fan et al., 2012b</a>), indicating a highly active starch catabolism during the <a id="xref-def-1-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> process (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). Three transcripts encoding phosphoglucomutase (g7581) and ADP-Glc pyrophosphorylase (g5866 and g9503), which define the beginning committed steps of starch biosynthesis, were down-regulated by the end of heterotrophy (72 h). Two granule-bound starch synthase I genes (g2429 and g7634) showed continuous up-regulation during the process of light induction (72–96 h). Transcripts responsible for starch breakdown, including glucan-water dikinase (g5653, g5659, and g7110), β-amylase (g3412 and g9517), phosphoglucan water dikinase (g4469 and g8607), and starch phosphorylase (g4743 and g4758), showed remarkable increases both in the heterotrophic (24–72 h) and phototrophic (74–96 h) phases. The enzyme activities of both ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase and β-amylase showed the consistent trend of starch metabolism (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S10</a>). Thus, the redirection of carbon flux away from storage carbohydrates in dark-grown cells at 72 h suggests that starch serves as a carbon and energy supply when external supplies of organic carbon are depleted after the end of heterotrophy.</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Genomic and transcriptomic features of central carbon metabolism in C. pyrenoidosa. Genes encoding the enzymes of these pathways are labeled in red. The full names of the corresponding genes are given in Supplemental Table S9." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1921510622" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Genomic and transcriptomic features of central carbon metabolism in &lt;em&gt;C. pyrenoidosa&lt;/em&gt;. The full names of the corresponding genes are given in Supplemental Table S9.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F6.medium.gif" width="396" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 6." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F6.medium.gif" width="396" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F6.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/4212" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 6.</span> <p id="p-46" class="first-child">Genomic and transcriptomic features of central carbon metabolism in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>.
