The full names of the corresponding genes are given in <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S9</a>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h4>How Was Lipid Synthesized?</h4><p id="p-47">The heterotrophy-to-photoautotrophy change of cultivation mode increased cellular lipid content but reduced starch content in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> (<a id="xref-fig-1-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>) by an unknown mechanism. Glc was exhausted at the end of the heterotrophic process, and sudden illumination was supplied subsequently. Accordingly, genes encoding enzymes associated with fatty acid and neutral lipid metabolic pathways were differentially expressed (<a id="xref-fig-7-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 7</a>). Of the four genes encoding subunits of heteromeric <a id="xref-def-5-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">ACCase</a>, two (biotin carboxylase [g8911] and biotin carboxyl carrier protein [g2736]) decreased transcriptionally at the end of heterotrophic phase and then recovered quickly following 2 h of light induction. Enzyme activity of <a id="xref-def-5-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-5">ACCase</a> confirmed its up-regulation during the process (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S10</a>). Similar patterns were also observed in fatty acid synthetase genes, including <em>MALONYL-COA:ACP TRANSACYLASE, BETA KETOACYL-ACP SYNTHASE, BETA KETOACYL-ACP REDUCTASE, BETA-HYDROXYACYL-ACP DEHYDRATASE, and ENOYL-ACP REDUCTASE</em> (<a id="xref-fig-7-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F7">Fig. 7</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S9</a>). Thus, de novo fatty acid synthesis is modulated by light/dark conditions, with synthesis increasing in the light and decreasing in the dark (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">Sasaki et al., 1997</a>).</p><div id="F7" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F7.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Fatty acid and glycerolipid biosynthesis and regulation in C. pyrenoidosa FACHB-9 as reconstructed from genomic and transcriptomic evidence. Metabolic steps are represented by arrows. Dashed lines represent the presence of multiple metabolic steps. End products are shown in boxes. Genes encoding the enzymes in these pathways are labeled in red. Up- or down-regulation of mRNA expression under heterotrophic growth (compared with heterotrophy 0 h, leftward arrows) and the heterotrophy to photoautotrophy transition (compared with heterotrophy 72 h, rightward arrows) based on mRNA-seq data are indicated with red upward arrows and green downward arrows, respectively. The full names of the corresponding genes are given in Supplemental Table S9. Because the glycolytic enolase is coded by only one gene (g2571) in C. pyrenoidosa, it is proposed that in Chlorella spp., the glycolytic enolase is only active in the cytosol, resulting in a specific pattern of carbon flow following fixation that involves export of carbon from the plastid and then reimport for pyruvate generation." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-1921510622" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Fatty acid and glycerolipid biosynthesis and regulation in &lt;em&gt;C. pyrenoidosa&lt;/em&gt; FACHB-9 as reconstructed from genomic and transcriptomic evidence. Metabolic steps are represented by arrows. Dashed lines represent the presence of multiple metabolic steps. End products are shown in boxes. Genes encoding the enzymes in these pathways are labeled in red. Up- or down-regulation of mRNA expression under heterotrophic growth (compared with heterotrophy 0 h, leftward arrows) and the heterotrophy to photoautotrophy transition (compared with heterotrophy 72 h, rightward arrows) based on mRNA-seq data are indicated with red upward arrows and green downward arrows, respectively. The full names of the corresponding genes are given in Supplemental Table S9. Because the glycolytic enolase is coded by only one gene (g2571) in &lt;em&gt;C. pyrenoidosa&lt;/em&gt;, it is proposed that in &lt;em&gt;Chlorella&lt;/em&gt; spp., the glycolytic enolase is only active in the cytosol, resulting in a specific pattern of carbon flow following fixation that involves export of carbon from the plastid and then reimport for pyruvate generation.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 7." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F7.medium.gif" width="440" height="266"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 7." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F7.medium.gif" width="440" height="266"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F7.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 7." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F7.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/4173" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 7.</span> <p id="p-48" class="first-child">Fatty acid and glycerolipid biosynthesis and regulation in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 as reconstructed from genomic and transcriptomic evidence. Metabolic steps are represented by arrows. Dashed lines represent the presence of multiple metabolic steps. End products are shown in boxes. Genes encoding the enzymes in these pathways are labeled in red. Up- or down-regulation of mRNA expression under heterotrophic growth (compared with heterotrophy 0 h, leftward arrows) and the heterotrophy to photoautotrophy transition (compared with heterotrophy 72 h, rightward arrows) based on mRNA-seq data are indicated with red upward arrows and green downward arrows, respectively. The full names of the corresponding genes are given in <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S9</a>. Because the glycolytic enolase is coded by only one gene (g2571) in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>, it is proposed that in <em>Chlorella</em> spp., the glycolytic enolase is only active in the cytosol, resulting in a specific pattern of carbon flow following fixation that involves export of carbon from the plastid and then reimport for pyruvate generation.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-49">Transferring the dark-grown cells (72 h) to light conditions (80 h) resulted in a slight increase in transcript abundance of acyl-ACP thioesterase (g1189), suggesting increased fatty acid export from the plastid to the endoplasmic reticulum, where TAG assembly occurs. Among the six putative <em>LC-FACS</em> genes identified in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> genome data set, two were almost completely suppressed under either or both growth conditions (<a id="xref-def-10-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">FPKM</a> &lt; 5; g2654 and g6978; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S9</a>). Another three genes showed a 4.4- to 17.5-fold decrease in mRNA abundance in the dark. Two of the three, g3983 and g9628, were up-regulated under light illumination; the remaining gene (g6510) showed an increase in transcript abundance at the log phase upon heterotrophic growth. These observations suggest distinct roles for individual <em>LC-FACS</em> genes and the presence of an intricate regulatory mechanism.</p><p id="p-50">Transcriptional levels of TAG assembly pathways, including genes encoding glycerol-3-phosphate dehydrogenase (g2897 and g5566), lysophospholipid acyltransferase (LPAAT; g3070 and g9996), phosphatidic acid phosphatase (g6947), and diacylglycerol acyltransferase (type-2 DGAT; g3280 and g7566) showed a synchronized increase by the end of heterotrophic growth (72 h). In <em>Chlamydomonas</em> and <em>Nannochloropsis</em> spp., transcriptome and genetic analysis revealed contribution of acyltransferases to TAG accumulation and their role as potential targets for manipulating TAG hyperaccumulation (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">Boyle et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">Sanjaya et al., 2013</a>; <a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">Li et al., 2014a</a>). Several carotenoid biosynthesis genes exhibited similar expression patterns (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S3</a>); one exception is that a LPAAT gene (g8553), predicted to function in the chloroplast prokaryotic pathway, was down-regulated at 72 h in dark-grown cells and then recovered by light induction. Thus, TAG accumulation, which usually accompanies secondary carotenoid synthesis (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">Rabbani et al., 1998</a>; <a id="xref-ref-89-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-89">Zhekisheva et al., 2002</a>), might be regulated by the heterotrophy-to-autotrophy switch, thus protecting the chloroplast from potential photooxidative damage. Therefore, the dynamic modulation of eukaryotic and prokaryotic pathways for glycerolipid synthesis, which accompanies the heterotrophy-to-photoautotrophy switch, may be crucial for forming the various intracellular energy storage compounds.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h4>Dynamics of the Central Metabolism That Supports the Switch</h4><p id="p-51">Central metabolism pathways played a prominent role in the metabolic switch of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> (<a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). Key enzymes from the tricarboxylic acid (<a id="xref-def-7-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a>) cycle), respiratory chain, and oxidative phosphorylation were transcriptionally up-regulated at the 24-h time point in the dark (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S3</a>). The energy economy of cell growth is dominated by ATP production and the reducing agent NADH via the <a id="xref-def-7-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a> cycle, which supports various metabolic processes. Consistent with the enrichment of starch granules at the beginning of the heterotrophic phase (<a id="xref-ref-17-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">Fan et al., 2012b</a>), up-regulation of gluconeogenesis genes was evident within the first 24 h of heterotrophy, which directed carbon from central carbon metabolism to intracellular carbohydrate stores. One Fru-bisphosphate aldolase (g6760) and two glyceraldehyde-3-P dehydrogenase genes (g4391 and g3826) were up-regulated 3- to 16-fold (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S3</a>). Although these enzymes can also catalyze the reversible reactions of glycolysis, transcripts of a phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (g2374), one pyruvate phosphate dikinase (g7054), and three Fru 1, 6-bisphosphatase genes (g5024, g4411, and g3655) also increased markedly.</p><p id="p-52">The glyoxylate cycle serves as a shunt of the <a id="xref-def-7-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a> cycle and allows cells to utilize simple carbon compounds as carbon source when complex sources such as Glc are unavailable (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">Lorenz and Fink, 2002</a>). This permits the synthesis of Glc from lipids via acetate generated in fatty acid β-oxidation. Transcripts of isocitrate lyases (g1361 and g4926) and malate synthases (g10080), which are specific to the glyoxylate cycle, showed a 3- to 25-fold increase at the end of the heterotrophic culture conditions (72 h). Accordingly, transcripts of acetyl-CoA synthetase (ACS; g7446, g7414, and g4467) increased 7- to 12-fold at the end of the heterotrophic process. ACS catalyzes acetate to produce acetyl-CoA, a precursor in the glyoxylate cycle, <a id="xref-def-7-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a> cycle, and fatty acid biosynthesis. In contrast to the initial heterotrophic phase, majority of the transcripts for key enzymes of glycolysis, gluconeogenesis, Calvin and <a id="xref-def-7-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a> cycles, and the pentose phosphate pathway declined at the end of heterotrophic process (i.e., after 72 h, when Glc became limiting), exhibiting a global down-regulation of central carbon metabolism (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Sets S3 and S4</a>).</p><p id="p-53">In addition, oxidative pentose phosphate (OPP) pathway genes, including 6-phosphogluconate dehydrogenase (decarboxylating; g4585) and 6-phosphogluconolactonase (g3624), were transcriptionally up-regulated approximately 3-fold under light conditions (80 h). The elevated OPP activity resulted in the generation of reducing equivalents (in the form of NADPH) that drive reductive biosynthesis reactions (e.g., fatty acid synthesis); thus, up-regulation of the OPP pathway may eventually contribute to lipid accumulation. The pyruvate dehydrogenase complex (PDC) subunits (g2537, g9493, g1999, and g1804) were also up-regulated following 2-h light illumination (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S4</a>). The PDC transforms pyruvate into acetyl-CoA, which may then be used in fatty acid biosynthesis or as an input to the <a id="xref-def-7-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a> cycle to carry out cellular respiration. Five fatty acid biosynthesis genes (g4578, g2736, g8879, g8526, and g8911) were rapidly up-regulated upon transferring cells from dark conditions to photoautotrophy (from 72–80 h; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S4</a>), suggesting that carbon from photosynthesis or glycolysis might be channeled through acetyl-CoA and then into this pathway. Furthermore, changes of enzymatic activities of the key metabolic notes, including those from glycolysis, <a id="xref-def-7-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a> cycle, gluconeogenesis, and oxidative pentose phosphate pathway, were consistent with the transcriptome analysis (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S10</a>). Collectively, these findings reveal the complex interactions of the central metabolic pathways that jointly support the switch.</p></div><div id="sec-14" class="subsection"><h4>Reboot of Photosynthetic Pathways Promoted the Switch</h4><p id="p-54">Down-regulation of photosynthesis is one response to external shocks in microalgae such as nutrient deficiency, stress, or nutritional alteration (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">González-Ballester et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">Miller et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">Xiong et al., 2010</a>; <a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">Msanne et al., 2012</a>; <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">Fan et al., 2014a</a>, <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">2014b</a>; <a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">Schmollinger et al., 2014</a>). Most of the photosystem proteins, light-harvesting complexes, and the cytochrome <em>b</em><sub>6</sub><em>f</em> complex were not down-regulated until 72 h in darkness (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S3</a>). In contrast to their inert and decreased expression in heterotrophy (24 and 72 h), most of the photosynthesis-related genes showed a fast response, reaching their maximal expression levels at 2 h after photoautotrophy began (74 h; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S4</a>), indicating that <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> is able to rapidly reboot its photosynthetic apparatus to protect from photo damage after the onset of light.</p><p id="p-55">Notably, carbon fixation genes showed a moderate response during heterotrophy (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S9</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S3</a>). By contrast, a rapid increase in the transcript abundance of predicted chloroplast Calvin cycle genes was observed at the onset of light induction (74 h; <a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S4</a>). Moreover, up-regulation was observed for C4 dicarboxylic acid cycle enzymes, including NADP-malate dehydrogenase (NADP-MDH; g4904), carbonic anhydrase (g5902), pyruvate phosphate dikinase (PPDK; g7054), and phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (g2374), following the nutritional conversion from heterotrophy to photoautotrophy (74–96 h; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S4</a>). NADP-MDH and PPDK are both reported to be light-modulated enzymes that function in the C4 pathway of photosynthesis (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">Hatch and Slack, 1969</a>; <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">Carr et al., 1999</a>; <a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">Ocheretina et al., 2000</a>). Thus, nimble response of the carbon fixation pathway genes might underpin rapid adaptation of the microalga to the phototrophic culture.</p></div></div><div id="sec-15" class="subsection"><h3>Genetic Transformation of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 Using Electroporation</h3><p id="p-56">To demonstrate amenability of industrial production <em>Chlorella</em> spp. strains to genetic engineering, electroporation of FACHB-9 was performed without prior enzymatic treatments and selection on 25 mg L<sup>–1</sup> G418. A transformation rate of about 1.07 × 10<sup>–5</sup> per microgram plasmid DNA was achieved (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Methods S1</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S11</a>). The efficiency of the transformations was strongly affected by the promoter and field strength (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S11</a>), as the highest number of colonies was generated using 3,300 V cm<sup>–1</sup> field strength, and the promoter producing the highest number of transformants was <em>Heat shock protein70</em> (<em>Hsp70</em>; followed by <em>Tubulin</em>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Table S11</a>). Both genomic PCR and Southern blot confirmed presence and integration of the transgenes in selected colonies (<a id="xref-fig-8-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F8">Fig. 8, B and C</a>). Fluorescence microscopy observation analysis confirmed successful stable expression of the exogenous GFP gene in resistant <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> lines (<a id="xref-fig-8-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F8">Fig. 8, D and E</a>). Stable integration and expression of the exogenous gene were confirmed after five generations of growth via antibiotic selection in both agar plates and liquid media.</p><div id="F8" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F8.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Transformation of C. pyrenoidosa FACHB-9 and validation of transgenic strains. D, Identification of the transgenic strains using fluorescence microscopy observation. E, Identification of the negative control (which underwent the entire electroporation protocol without any plasmid DNA) using fluorescence microscopy.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-16" class="subsection"><h3>Overexpression of the <em>AtNADK3</em> Significantly Enhances the Fatty Acids and Lipid Content in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9</h3><p id="p-58">Our transcriptome analysis indicated the switch from heterotrophy to light environment might lead to generation of reducing energy equivalents (e.g., in the form of NADPH) that drive reductive biosynthesis reactions (e.g., fatty acid synthesis). Thus, increasing the level of the intracellular reducing equivalents may eventually contribute to lipid accumulation. In Arabidopsis, NAD(H) kinase3, a key source of the cellular reductant NADPH, is required for various abiotic stress responses (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Chai et al., 2006</a>). The corresponding gene <em>AtNADK3</em> encodes a NADH kinase that can synthesize NADPH with NADH as the preferred substrate and also utilizes NAD+ (while the other two <em>AtNADK</em> genes can utilize only singular substrate; <a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">Chai et al., 2006</a>). Intriguingly, in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 genome, only one endogenous copy of NADK (g6804) was found. Transcriptome analysis showed that the <em>NADK3</em> native to <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> was up-regulated by 360% and 41.6% in the heterotrophic and light induction process (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Data Set S1</a>), suggesting an active role of this transcript in lipid production. To test whether higher dose of this enzyme could lead to NADPH accumulation and thus promote fatty acid synthesis, we introduced into FACHB-9 one more copy of <em>AtNADK3</em> using the genetic transformation system we developed (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Methods S1</a>).</p><p id="p-59">Growth curves of the transgenic <em>AtNADK3</em> strains and wild-type <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> were similar at either heterotrophy or photoautotrophy (<a id="xref-fig-9-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 9, A and B</a>), suggesting absence of obvious deleterious effects on growth as introduced by the transformation. Because <em>Hsp70</em> promoter, as a transcriptional enhancer, promoted transgene expression in <em>C. reinhardtii</em> under inducing conditions (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">Rosales-Mendoza et al., 2012</a>), we compared three culture conditions (heterotrophy, photoinduction, and heat shock) to probe whether the native <em>Hsp70</em> promoter works as constitutive or inducible type in <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>. All the three conditions induced <em>AtNADK3</em> expression, yet with distinct effects (<a id="xref-fig-9-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 9C</a>; <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Fig. S10</a>). Compared with the wild type, all three transgenic strains exhibited significantly increased NADPH and lipid content, by 39.3% to 79.9% (<a id="xref-fig-9-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 9D</a>) and 45.3% to 110.4% (<a id="xref-fig-9-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 9E</a>), respectively. Main fatty acid components of the transgenic strain NADK3-2 and the wild type were similar, as revealed by gas chromatography-mass spectrometry analysis (<a id="xref-fig-9-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 9F</a>). Both light induction and heat shock increased the cellular content of the fatty acids (<a id="xref-fig-9-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 9F</a>) compared with the wild type, yet the relative compositions of total fatty acids were not significantly different from that in the wild type (<a id="xref-fig-9-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F9">Fig. 9F</a>). Among the three investigated induction strategies, light was the most effective way (followed by heat shock and then heterotrophy) to induce both gene expression and NADPH accumulation as well as lipid biosynthesis (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Figs. S10 and S11</a>).</p><div id="F9" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F9.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Characterization of AtNADK3-overexpressing C. pyrenoidosa FACHB-9 strains under different culture conditions. For light induction (100 &#x3BC;mol m&#x2013;2 s&#x2013;1) and heat shock (42&#xB0;C), 24-h cultivation was carried out using heterotrophic cells as seed. DCW, Dry cell weight; WT, wild-type &lt;em&gt;C. pyrenoidosa&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Figure 9." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F9.medium.gif" width="336" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Figure 9." src="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F9.medium.gif" width="336" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F9.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Figure 9." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li><li class="new-tab"><a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/content/plantphysiol/169/4/2444/F9.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li><li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/4404" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li></ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Figure 9.</span> <p id="p-60" class="first-child">Characterization of <em>AtNADK3</em>-overexpressing <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 strains under different culture conditions. A, Growth curves of three <em>AtNADK3</em>-overexpressing strains under heterotrophic culture conditions. B, Growth curves of the three <em>AtNADK3</em>-overexpressing strains under photoautotrophic culture conditions. C, Relative expression levels of <em>AtNADK3</em> in the NADK3-2 strain under different culture conditions. D, NADPH content of the three <em>AtNADK3</em>-overexpressing strains under different culture conditions. E, Total lipid contents of the three <em>AtNADK3</em>-overexpressing strains under different culture conditions. F, The fatty acids content and composition in the NADK3-2 strain under different culture conditions. Data represent the means ± <span class="sc">sd</span> of three biological replicate experiments and were analyzed by Student’s <em>t</em> test (<em>n</em> = 3). Asterisks indicate a significant difference from wild-type strains (**, <em>P</em> &lt; 0.01). For heterotrophy, 72-h cultivation was carried out. For light induction (100 μmol m<sup>–2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) and heat shock (42°C), 24-h cultivation was carried out using heterotrophic cells as seed. DCW, Dry cell weight; WT, wild-type <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-61">Overexpression of <em>NADK</em> causes perturbation of NADP(H) pool and has positive effects on growth or stress tolerance (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">Pollak et al., 2007</a>; <a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">Panagiotou et al., 2009</a>), e.g., stimulation of photosynthesis metabolism and tolerance of oxidative damages in higher plants (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">Takahashi et al., 2009</a>; <a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">Takahara et al., 2010</a>). Our study is, to our knowledge, the first to show that the NADP(H) pool can be engineered to increase lipid content, by introducing a single NAD(H) kinase gene in microalgae. Thus, these findings suggest a new strategy for strain development for higher lipid productivity in not just industrial microalgae but also higher plants.</p></div></div><div class="section conclusions" id="sec-17"><h2 class="">CONCLUSION</h2><p id="p-62">The green algae <em>Chlorella</em> spp. are among the most widely cultivated microalgae in the world (<a id="xref-ref-30-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">Klein-Marcuschamer et al., 2013</a>). Genome of the industrial strain <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 revealed positive selection and gene family expansion in lipid and carbohydrate metabolism genes, as well as those related to the cell cycle and response to stimuli. <a id="xref-def-3-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-3">HGT</a>s were inferred in several lipid metabolism nodes. Temporal analysis of transcriptomes during the metabolic switch from a starch-rich heterotrophy mode to a protein- and lipid-rich photoautotrophy mode revealed a marked redirection of metabolism in which the primary carbon source, Glc, is no longer supplied to cell building blocks by the <a id="xref-def-7-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-7">TCA</a> cycle and gluconeogenesis, while carbon skeletons from photosynthesis and starch degradation may be channeled directly into fatty acid and protein biosynthesis. By establishing, to our knowledge, the first genetic transformation for industrial <em>Chlorella</em> spp., we show that overexpression of the <em>AtNADK3</em> significantly enhances the fatty acids and lipid content without affecting its growth. This is, to our knowledge, the first report of increasing microalgal fatty acid and lipid content by genetically engineering cellular-reducing equivalents. These findings thus serve as a foundation for engineering the metabolic switch apparatus in this and related algae for optimized and controllable production of food and fuels.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-18"><h2 class="">MATERIALS AND METHODS</h2><p id="p-63">For a detailed description of all methods, see <a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Methods S1</a>.</p><div id="sec-19" class="subsection"><h3>Strain and Culture Conditions</h3><p id="p-64"><em>Chlorella pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 (Freshwater Algae Culture Collection of the Institute of Hydrobiology, Chinese Academy of Sciences) was cultured heterotrophically in Endo medium (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">Endo et al., 1974</a>) in flasks or in 50- and 500-L fermenters with Glc and urea as the organic carbon and nitrogen source. F-Si medium (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">Sato et al., 2006</a>) was chosen for photoautotrophic culture lipid production with 2% (v/v) CO<sub>2</sub>/air supplementation.</p><p id="p-65">For <a id="xref-def-1-10" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> cultivation, the heterotrophically grown cells (in which Glc was already depleted) were resuspended in water at a final cell concentration of about 2 g L<sup>–1</sup> and then transferred to 5-d phototrophic cultivation under indoor continuous light at approximately 300 μmol m<sup>–2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at 30°C. For each of cultivation processes above, samples were taken at specific intervals to determine biomass and cellular component production.</p></div><div id="sec-20" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing and Assembly</h3><p id="p-66">For <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> FACHB-9 genome sequencing, we employed Roche 454 Titanium to collect single-end and paired-end reads. We first generated a total of 3,730,590 454-Titanium sequence reads (average read length, approximately 400 bp, with pair-end distances of 3, 8, and 20 kb, respectively). Assembly of the single- and pair-ended 454 sequences (1.46 Gbp in total and approximately 26-fold coverage) with Newbler version 2.0 (Roche) resulted in 8,193 contigs that assembled into 1,345 scaffolds longer than 2 kb. The assembled scaffolds were further screened and filtered by searching against all bacterial sequences from the SILVA (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">Pruesse et al., 2007</a>) and National Center for Biotechnology Information (<a id="xref-def-8-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">NCBI</a>) nr database to filter out possible contaminants. The resultant assembly of the <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> nuclear genome contains 1,336 scaffolds totaling 56.8 Mbp in size, with a contig N/L50 of 1,265/11 kb and a scaffold N/L50 of 9/1.39 Mbp.</p><p id="p-67">Algal cells grown under different heterotrophic and phototrophic conditions (±Glc, low-/high-light illumination, and logarithmic/stationary phase) were collected and harvested by centrifugation. Each sample was then pooled to prepare libraries of <a id="xref-def-2-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a> for mRNA sequencing on 454 Titanium (Roche). A total of 533,300 raw reads were produced using GS-Titanium. All raw reads were trimmed based on quality value before further analysis. All <a id="xref-def-2-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a> reads that passed quality control were used for transcript-based gene prediction. About 81% (8,317 genes) of the total number of predicted protein-coding genes were covered by the 454 cDNA sequencing reads.</p></div><div id="sec-21" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Annotation and Analyses</h3><p id="p-68">The assembled draft nuclear genome of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> was annotated using the QIBEBT-FUNGEA annotation pipeline (accessibility), which combines three different ab initio gene predictors (Augustus version 2.5 [<a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">Stanke and Morgenstern, 2005</a>], GeneID v1.2 [<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">Parra et al., 2000</a>], and SNAP [<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">Korf, 2004</a>]) and one evidence-based gene predictor (Cufflinks version 1.1.0 [<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Trapnell et al., 2010</a>]). The <a id="xref-def-2-6" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a> reads that passed quality control were also used for transcript-based gene prediction. Functional annotation of the predicted protein-coding genes was carried out based on the identification of protein domains using InterProScan (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">Quevillon et al., 2005</a>) database and BLASTp matches against the <a id="xref-def-8-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">NCBI</a> nr, Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) and Conserved Domain Database (CDD) databases, with an E-value &lt; 10<sup>–5</sup>. Protein sequences from <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> were used as BLASTp queries against the sequenced alga species, and hits with an E-value &lt; 10<sup>–10</sup> were considered homologous proteins. For selection pressure analysis of protein-coding genes in green algae, orthologs among seven selected Chlorophyta strains were first identified by a Markov Clustering algorithm (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">Li et al., 2003</a>). PAML (<a id="xref-ref-84-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-84">Yang, 2007</a>) codon substitution models and likelihood ratio tests (codeml) were then used to estimate the rate of evolution and to test the selection pressure. To connect gene function with sequence evolution, <a id="xref-def-4-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-4">GO</a> term assignments for each of the genes were retrieved from InterproScan results.</p></div><div id="sec-22" class="subsection"><h3>RNA-Seq and Differential Expression Analysis</h3><p id="p-69">Total RNA was extracted from <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> cells during the <a id="xref-def-1-11" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> process. Briefly, the <a id="xref-def-1-12" class="xref-list" href="#def-1">SHDP</a> cultivations were performed in 500-mL shaker flasks. Algal cells were first cultivated heterotrophically to achieve high cell density. After Glc was completely consumed, the broth was diluted to approximately 2 g L<sup>–1</sup> dry biomass and transferred to a light environment for photoautotrophic culture. Three separate flasks (i.e., three biological replicates) were analyzed in parallel (<a href="http://www.plantphysiol.org/cgi/content/full/pp.15.01174/DC1">Supplemental Methods S1</a>). As a result, 18 samples in total, with the three biological replicates collected from each of the six time points (0, 24, and 72 h from heterotrophic cells and 2, 8, and 24 h from phototrophic cells; <a id="xref-fig-1-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1C</a>), were used for mRNA-seq library preparation and then submitted to Solexa GA-IIx (Illumina) for sequencing (short insert [approximately 400 bp] paired-end, 2 × 100 and 2 × 90 bp). Default parameters were used to screen reads using the Illumina quality control pipeline. The processed sequence files (a total of 187,503,403 reads) were then mapped to the assembled genome by TopHat version 2.0.4 (<a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">Trapnell et al., 2009</a>) with a tolerance of up to two mismatches and three indels. For each of the RNA-seq data sets, the transcript relative abundance (<a id="xref-def-9-1" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">TRA</a>) was expressed as the number of aligned reads to annotated gene models using Cufflinks (version 1.1.0; <a id="xref-ref-78-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">Trapnell et al., 2010</a>) and then normalized to <a id="xref-def-10-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">FPKM</a> values. Fold expression changes between different time points were calculated using the <a id="xref-def-9-2" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">TRA</a> log<sub>2</sub> ratios. Genes were regarded as differentially expressed if they had at least a 3-fold change and 5% or less false discovery rate. Furthermore, the gene sets with positive <a id="xref-def-9-3" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">TRA</a> log<sub>2</sub> ratios that had numerators greater than 50 <a id="xref-def-10-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">FPKM</a> were regarded as up-regulated. Down-regulated genes were those with negative <a id="xref-def-9-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-9">TRA</a> log<sub>2</sub> ratios that had denominators greater than 50 <a id="xref-def-10-5" class="xref-list" href="#def-10">FPKM</a>.</p></div><div id="sec-23" class="subsection"><h3>Nuclear Transformation by Electroporation</h3><p id="p-70">FACHB-9 cells were cultured up to logarithmic phase, harvested following centrifugation at 3,000 rpm for 10 min, and then processed with a high-exudate buffer containing 0.2 <span class="sc">m</span> sorbitol and mannitol on ice. The processed cells were recentrifuged at 3,000 rpm and resuspended with a buffer at a concentration of approximately 5 × 10<sup>6</sup> cells mL<sup>–1</sup> and then immediately mixed with a final concentration of 30 µg mL<sup>–1</sup> plasmid pGreen0029-Ubi-eGFP-Nos (freely available by contacting Jianhua Fan), a final concentration of 10 μg mL<sup>–1</sup> plasmid pSoup, and 25 μg mL<sup>–1</sup> salmon sperm DNA. The mixture was placed on ice for 10 min before a 100-μL mixture was supplied into the 2-mm electroporation cuvette for transformation with a Bio-Rad Gene Pulser II Electroporation System. The cells were transformed at a field strength of 1.8 to 4.3 kV cm<sup>–1</sup> leading to time constants of 1 to 5 ms. Following electroporation, cells were screened via selective medium (in agar plates with 25 mg L<sup>–1</sup> G418; <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">Nichols, 1973</a>) and validated.</p></div><div id="sec-24" class="subsection"><h3>Construction, Validation, and Phenotyping of <em>AtNADK3</em>-Overexpression Strains of <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em></h3><p id="p-71">To test whether higher dose of this enzyme could lead to NADPH accumulation and thus promote fatty acid synthesis, we introduced into FACHB-9 one more copy of <em>AtNADK3</em> using the genetic transformation system we developed. The <a id="xref-def-2-7" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a> of <em>AtNADK3</em> was amplified from the leaves of Arabidopsis (<em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>) according to the published <em>AtNADK3</em> <a id="xref-def-2-8" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a> sequence (<a id="xref-def-8-4" class="xref-list" href="#def-8">NCBI</a> accession no. <a class="external-ref external-ref-type-gen" href="/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=NM_106506&amp;atom=%2Fplantphysiol%2F169%2F4%2F2444.atom">NM_106506</a>), and the fragments were then inserted into the site between <em>Spe</em>I and <em>Not</em>I in the plastid pGreen0029-Ubi-eGFP-Nos to replace <em>eGFP</em>. Similarly, the promoter <em>Ubiquitin1</em> gene was also replaced with endogenous promoter with <em>Hin</em>dIII and <em>Bam</em>HI to create the pGreen0029-Hsp70-AtNADK3-Nos.</p><p id="p-72">Fatty acid composition of mutant cells was qualitatively and quantitatively determined using a gas chromatography mass spectrometer. For gene expression, the first-strand <a id="xref-def-2-9" class="xref-list" href="#def-2">cDNA</a> synthesis and real-time quantitative PCR were performed using the ReverTra Ace qPCR RT Master Mix with gDNA Remover and SYBR Green Realtime PCR Master Mix (Toyobo), respectively. A pair of specific primers (forward, ATCGCATCCCATTCGTGAT, and reverse, TGCCAAACAAAATGTCATCCA) were designed and used for expression-level quantitative detection. The comparative threshold cycle method was used to analyze gene expression relative to control based on the average fold change. The content of NADPH was determined using the coenzyme II NADP (H) content kit (SuZhou Comin).</p><p id="p-73">The <em>C. pyrenoidosa</em> whole-genome sequencing project (PRJNA171991) has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession number ANZC00000000. The version described in this paper is the first version, ANZC01000000. The mRNA-seq raw data are available at the Gene Expression Omnibus database (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/</a>) under the accession number GSE40028. The genome and transcriptome data can also be freely accessed through the project Web site (<a href="http://www.bioenergychina.org:8989/indexs/Chlorella_pyrenoidosa_index.html">http://www.bioenergychina.org:8989/indexs/Chlorella_pyrenoidosa_index.html</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-25" class="subsection"><h3>Supplemental Data</h3><p id="p-74">The following supplemental materials are available.</p><ul class="list-simple " id="list-1"><li id="list-item-1"><p id="p-75"><a         <h2 class="pane-title">In this issue</h2>

    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-article-citation" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-issue" data-node-nid="31" id="node31" data-pisa="plantphysiol;169/4" data-pisa-master="plantphysiol;169/4" data-apath="/plantphysiol/169/4.atom"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-issue highwire-citation-jcore-issue-widget clearfix">

  <div class="highwire-cite-col">
